Handle duplicates, missing values, inconsistent formatting, outliers, and text processing issues that prevent downstream statistical analysis.\nskillx.scope_in: |\n  - Deduplication based on logical keys and downstream requirements\n  - Missing value handling (drop vs impute) based on column criticality and sample size impact\n  - Text processing (extract numbers, clean whitespace, standardize formats)\n  - Outlier handling (cap vs remove) based on statistical methods and downstream sensitivity\n  - Data type conversions and date parsing for time-series work\n  - Ensuring referential integrity across related datasets (e.g., survey IDs linking to transactions)\nskillx.scope_out: |\n  - Feature engineering or derived variable creation (separate step)\n  - Aggregation or reshaping for specific analyses (e.g., DID panel structure)\n  - Statistical modeling or causal inference itself\n  - Data validation rules or schema enforcement beyond basic type checking\n  - Performance optimization for very large datasets (>10M rows)\nskillx.requires: |\n  - pandas for dataframe operations\n  - Understanding of the downstream task to make informed cleaning decisions\n  - Knowledge of which columns are critical vs optional for the analysis\n  - Sample size considerations (cost of dropping rows vs imputing)\nskillx.preferred_tools: |\n  - `df.drop_duplicates(subset=...)` for deduplication with logical keys\n  - `df.dropna(subset=...)` for critical columns where imputation would harm analysis\n  - `df.fillna()` with median/mode for non-critical columns with sufficient independence\n  - `sklearn.impute.KNNImputer` when target column values can be inferred from related columns\n  - `pd.to_datetime()` with error handling for date columns\n  - IQR-based methods (winsorization or removal) for outlier handling\n  - String methods (`str.extract()`, `str.strip()`, `str.lower()`) for text cleaning\nskillx.risks: |\n  - Dropping too many rows due to aggressive missing value or outlier removal can harm sample size and statistical power\n  - Imputing critical outcome variables or treatment indicators can introduce bias\n  - Removing outliers without understanding if they represent real signal vs noise can distort conclusions\n  - Inconsistent cleaning logic between related datasets can break joins (e.g., survey IDs not matching transactions)\n  - Over-cleaning can remove legitimate variation needed for causal analysis\n  - Text processing that loses semantic information (e.g., extracting only numbers when categories matter)\nskillx.examples: |\n  Remove duplicates by logical key:\n  ```python\n  df.drop_duplicates(subset=['customer_id', 'order_date'], keep='first')\n  ```\n\n  Drop rows with missing critical identifiers:\n  ```python\n  df.dropna(subset=['customer_id', 'order_date'], inplace=False)\n  ```\n\n  Impute non-critical numerical columns with median:\n  ```python\n  df['income'].fillna(df['income'].median(), inplace=True)\n  ```\n\n  Cap outliers using IQR (winsorization to preserve sample size):\n  ```python\n  Q1, Q3 = df['amount'].quantile([0.25, 0.75])\n  IQR = Q3 - Q1\n  lower, upper = Q1 - 1.5*IQR, Q3 + 1.5*IQR\n  df['amount'] = df['amount'].clip(lower, upper)\n  ```\n---\n\n# Guidance\n\n## Decision Framework for Cleaning Operations\n\n**Deduplication**: Always check for duplicates first. Examine the data's logical structure and downstream goals:\n- For demographic/survey data: typically one row per respondent (duplicate Survey ResponseIDs should be removed)\n- For transaction data: multiple transactions per user are legitimate (do NOT remove based on user ID alone)\n- Use appropriate subset of columns that define uniqueness for the data type\n\n**Missing Value Strategy**:\n1. Assess criticality: Will missing values in this column directly harm downstream analysis performance?\n2. Assess cost: How many rows would be lost by dropping vs potential bias from imputing?\n3. Decision tree:\n   - Critical columns (IDs, dates, outcome variables): drop rows with missingness\n   - Non-critical numerical with sufficient independence: impute with median\n   - Non-critical categorical with sufficient independence: impute with mode\n   - Non-critical with strong relationships to other columns: consider KNN imputation\n4. For KNN imputation: only use when you can identify semantically related columns (e.g., income can be inferred from age + education)\n\n**Text Processing**: Preview column values to determine if cleaning is needed:\n- Extract numbers from mixed text/number fields if downstream requires numeric values\n- Standardize whitespace and case for categorical fields used in joins or grouping\n- Remove special characters only if they interfere with analysis (preserve meaning otherwise)\n\n**Outlier Handling**:\n1. Understand if outliers represent real signal or data quality issues\n2. Assess sample size impact of removal\n3. Decision tree:\n   - If sample size is large and outliers are clearly erroneous: remove using IQR or Z-score\n   - If sample size is precious or outliers might be real: cap using winsorization (IQR method)\n   - Default multiplier: 1.5 for IQR, 3.0 for Z-score\n4. For causal inference work: be cautious about removing extreme values that may represent legitimate treatment effects\n\n## Implementation Pattern\n\nFor complex cleaning pipelines:\n1. Read and preview data first (`df.head()`, `df.info()`, `df.describe()`)\n2. Check for duplicates and understand their source\n3. Identify missing value patterns (`df.isnull().sum()`)\n4. Clean in stages: identifiers → critical fields → non-critical fields → outliers\n5. Verify referential integrity between related datasets before proceeding to merges\n6. Document the number of rows affected by each operation\n7. Save cleaned outputs with clear naming (e.g., `survey_cleaned.csv`, not `survey_v2.csv`)\n\n## Common Patterns for This Task Domain\n\n**Survey/Demographic Data**:\n- Expect one row per respondent\n- Check for duplicate Survey ResponseIDs and remove\n- Demographic columns may have missingness requiring imputation\n- Verify all IDs follow expected format (e.g., start with 'R_')\n\n**Transaction/Purchase Data**:\n- Multiple transactions per user are normal (do NOT deduplicate by user)\n- Date columns must be parseable and within expected range\n- Price/amount columns should be positive and within reasonable bounds\n- Category columns should not have nulls (either drop rows or impute with mode)\n- Verify Survey ResponseIDs link to the demographic data\n\n**Cross-Dataset Validation**:\n- After cleaning both datasets, verify that transaction IDs exist in demographic data\n- Filter transactions to only include users present in cleaned demographics if required for analysis\n- Ensure date ranges align with analysis requirements (e.g., 2019-2020 for this task)\n\n# Notes for Agent\n\n1. **Always preview before cleaning**: Use `df.head()`, `df.info()`, `df.describe()`, and `df.isnull().sum()` to understand the data structure and quality issues before applying any cleaning operations.\n\n2. **Document your decisions**: For each cleaning step, note why you chose that approach (e.g., \"Dropping rows with missing Category because it's required for downstream aggregation and only affects 7% of data\").\n\n3. **Check referential integrity early**: Before proceeding to analysis steps, verify that IDs in transaction data exist in demographic data. This prevents downstream join issues.\n\n4. **Be conservative with outlier removal**: For causal inference tasks, extreme values may represent real effects. Prefer winsorization (capping) over removal unless outliers are clearly data errors.\n\n5. **Validate cleaning results**: After each major operation, check the resulting dataset size and summary statistics to ensure cleaning didn't inadvertently remove too much data or introduce new issues.\n\n6. **Save intermediate outputs**: Write cleaned datasets to the specified output paths as soon as cleaning is complete, before proceeding to analysis steps. This enables inspection and debugging.\n\n7. **Handle date parsing carefully**: Use `pd.to_datetime()` with `errors='coerce'` to identify unparseable dates, then decide whether to drop or fix them. Provides strategy-based cleaning operations including deduplication, missing value imputation, text processing, and outlier handling through a configurable pipeline.\n\n## Core Components\n\n### CleaningStrategies\nCollection of static methods for specific cleaning operations:\n\n#### Deduplication\n- `remove_duplicates(df, subset=None)` - Remove duplicate rows given the context of the prompt or the context of the dataset, Carefully evaluate if it still makes sense to allow duplicates exist in the downstream analysis. You should always check the data and remove them based on the logic of meta data and downstream goal.\n\n#### Missing Value Handling\nLogic: If the column is not critical to affect the dowstream task and the cost of dropping the whole line is not neccessary, you should try to impute them, otherwise you can drop.\n- `drop_missing(df, columns)` - Drop rows with missing critical values (that will affect the downstream task's peformance) in specified columns.\n- `impute_median(df, columns)` - Impute with median for numerical columns if the column is numerical data\n- `impute_knn(df, target_features, n_neighbors=5)` - KNN-based imputation such that the target column value potentially can be inferred by other columns. I.e. you have to carefully see the semantic and logical relationship between columns in terms of real life cases and make decisions to use this method for better data imputation performance.\n- `impute_mode(df, columns)` - Impute with mode for categorical columns if the target column is relatively independent to other columns and should just be filled with an acceptable value.\n\n#### Text Processing\n- `process_text(df, columns, operation)` - Text operations: some columns might need conduct text related processing to get numbers or remove unnecessary content, you need to dertermine if this is needed based on preview the column values regarding the down stream goal. (extract_numbers, clean_whitespace, extract_email, lowercase, remove_special)\n\n#### Outlier Handling\nLogic: Again you need to make sure if removing outliers is costly or not in terms of the sample size or critical columns under your investigation. Keep the rows by replacing the outlier value with a more reasonable value, or simply remove the whole row in one of the two ways if it barely has any impact.\n- `cap_outliers_iqr(df, columns, multiplier=1.5)` - Winsorization using IQR to keep reasonable signals of the data;\n- `remove_outliers_iqr(df, columns, multiplier=1.5)` - Remove outliers using IQR\n- `remove_outliers_zscore(df, columns, threshold=3.0)` - Remove outliers using Z-score\n\n### DataCleaningPipeline\nOrchestrates multiple cleaning steps with logging and progress tracking.\n\n## Usage\n\n```python\nfrom data_cleaning import CleaningStrategies, DataCleaningPipeline\n\n# Create pipeline\npipeline = DataCleaningPipeline(name=\"Transaction Data\")\n\n# Add cleaning steps\npipeline.add_step(\n    CleaningStrategies.remove_duplicates,\n    subset=['customer_id', 'order_date'],\n    description=\"Remove duplicate orders\"\n).add_step(\n    CleaningStrategies.drop_missing,\n    columns=['customer_id', 'amount'],\n    description=\"Drop rows with missing critical fields\"\n).add_step(\n    CleaningStrategies.impute_knn,\n    target_features={\n        'income': {'features': ['age', 'education'], 'type': 'numeric'}\n    },\n    description=\"KNN imputation for income\"\n).add_step(\n    CleaningStrategies.cap_outliers_iqr,\n    columns=['amount'],\n    multiplier=1.5,\n    description=\"Cap price outliers\"\n)\n\n# Execute pipeline\ndf_clean = pipeline.execute(raw_df, verbose=True)\n\n# Get execution log\nlog_df = pipeline.get_log()\n```\n\n## Input\n- Any tabular DataFrame requiring cleaning\n\n## Output\n- Cleaned DataFrame with execution log available\n\n## Best Practices\n- Chain multiple steps using method chaining\n- Use verbose=True to monitor pipeline progress\n- Review execution log for quality assurance\n- Extend CleaningStrategies with domain-specific methods as needed\n","numLines":85,"startLine":1,"totalLines":85}}}

Handle duplicates, missing values, inconsistent formatting, outliers, and text processing.\nskillx.scope_in: |\n  - Deduplication based on logical keys and downstream requirements\n  - Missing value handling (drop vs impute) based on column criticality\n  - Text processing (extract numbers, clean whitespace, standardize formats)\n  - Outlier handling (cap vs remove) based on statistical methods\n  - Data type conversions and date parsing for time-series work\n  - Knowledge of which columns are critical vs optional\nskillx.preferred_tools: |\n  - `df.drop_duplicates(subset=...)` for deduplication with logical keys\n  - `df.dropna(subset=...)` for critical columns\n  - `df.fillna()` with median/mode for non-critical columns\n  - `sklearn.impute.KNNImputer` when values can be inferred from related columns\n  - `pd.to_datetime()` with error handling for date columns\n  - IQR-based methods for outlier handling\n  - String methods (`str.extract()`, `str.strip()`, `str.lower()`) for text cleaning\nskillx.risks: |\n  - Dropping too many rows harms sample size and statistical power\n  - Imputing critical outcome variables or treatment indicators introduces bias\n  - Removing outliers without understanding signal vs noise distorts conclusions\n  - Inconsistent cleaning logic between datasets breaks joins\n  - Q1\n  lower, upper = Q1 - 1.5*IQR, Q3 + 1.5*IQR\n  df['amount'] = df['amount'].clip(lower, upper)\n  ```\n---\n\n# Guidance\n\n## Decision Framework\n\n**Deduplication**: Check for duplicates first. Examine logical structure and downstream goals:\n- Survey/demographic data: one row per respondent (remove duplicate IDs)\n- Transaction data: multiple transactions per user are legitimate (do NOT deduplicate by user alone)\n- Use appropriate subset of columns that define uniqueness\n\n**Missing Value Strategy**:\n1. Critical columns (IDs, dates, outcome variables): drop rows with missingness\n2. Non-critical numerical: impute with median\n3. Non-critical categorical: impute with mode\n4. Non-critical with strong relationships to other columns: consider KNN imputation\n\n**Outlier Handling**:\n- If sample size is large and outliers are clearly errors: remove using IQR or Z-score\n- If sample size is precious or outliers might be real: cap using winsorization\n- Default: 1.5 for IQR multiplier, 3.0 for Z-score\n- For causal inference: be cautious about removing extreme values\n\n## Implementation Pattern\n\n1. Read and preview data (`df.head()`, `df.info()`, `df.describe()`)\n2. Check for duplicates and understand their source\n3. Identify missing value patterns (`df.isnull().sum()`)\n4. Clean in stages: identifiers → critical fields → non-critical fields → outliers\n5. Verify referential integrity between related datasets\n6. Document rows affected by each operation\n7. Save cleaned outputs with clear naming\n\n## Common Patterns\n\n**Survey/Demographic Data**:\n- Expect one row per respondent\n- Remove duplicate Survey ResponseIDs\n- Demographic columns may need imputation\n- Verify IDs follow expected format\n\n**Transaction/Purchase Data**:\n- Multiple transactions per user are normal\n- Date columns must be parseable and within expected range\n- Price/amount should be positive and within reasonable bounds\n- Category columns should not have nulls\n- Verify Survey ResponseIDs link to demographic data\n\n**Cross-Dataset Validation**:\n- After cleaning, verify transaction IDs exist in demographic data\n- Filter transactions to only include users in cleaned demographics if required\n- Ensure date ranges align with analysis requirements\n\n# Execution Notes\n\n1. **Preview before cleaning**: Use `df.head()`, `df.info()`, `df.describe()` to understand data structure\n\n2. **Document decisions**: Note why you chose each approach (e.g., \"Dropping rows with missing Category because required for aggregation\")\n\n3. **Check referential integrity early**: Verify IDs in transaction data exist in demographic data before proceeding\n\n4. **Be conservative with outlier removal**: For causal inference, extreme values may represent real effects. scikit-learn.preprocessing for encoding and scaling\n  - Pipeline or sequential transformation pattern for reproducibility\nskillx.risks:\n  - Non-numeric features break downstream DiD regression\n  - Convert binary survey responses (Yes/No, True/False) to 0/1 integers\n  - One-hot encode nominal categories (race, education) creating binary dummies\n  - Label encode ordinal categories (income brackets, age ranges) preserving order\n  - Validate output: df.select_dtypes(include='number') should contain all non-ID columns\n---\n\n# Guidance\n\n## Workflow\n\n1. **Inspect Survey Data**: Examine column names, data types, and sample values to identify Survey ResponseID and demographic columns\n\n2. **Categorize Features by Type**:\n   - Binary: Yes/No, True/False → convert to 0/1\n   - Nominal categorical: Race, education (no natural order) → one-hot encode\n   - Ordinal categorical: Income brackets, age ranges → label/ordinal encode\n   - Numeric: Already numeric → may need scaling if used directly\n\n3. **Apply Transformations**:\n   - **Low cardinality (<10 categories)**: One-hot encoding\n   - **Medium cardinality (10-50)**: Label encoding or frequency encoding\n   - **High cardinality (>50)**: Consider hashing or dropping\n   - For ordered categories, use ordinal encoding preserving order\n\n4. **Validate Output Requirements**:\n   ```python\n   # All columns except ID must be numeric\n   feature_cols = [c for c in df.columns if c != 'Survey ResponseID']\n   non_numeric = [c for c in feature_cols if df[c].dtype not in ['int64', 'float64']]\n   assert len(non_numeric) == 0, f\"Non-numeric columns: {non_numeric}\"\n\n   # Check for constant features\n   for col in feature_cols:\n       if df[col].nunique() == 1:\n           print(f\"Warning: {col} is constant, consider removing\")\n   ```\n\n5. **Save Output**: CSV with [Survey ResponseID, FEATURE1, FEATURE2, ..., FEATUREn], all features numeric\n\n## Critical Requirements\n\n1. **ALL features MUST be numeric (int or float)** - No string, object, or boolean dtypes (except Survey ResponseID). Validate: `df.select_dtypes(include='number').shape[1] == df.shape[1] - 1`\n\n2. **Complete categorical encoding** - Every categorical column must be converted. Do not leave string columns in output\n\n3. **Feature validation checklist**:\n   - No missing values\n   - No constant columns (all features have variance)\n   - No infinite values\n   - Reasonable feature count (at least 20 expected)\n\n4. **Preserve data integrity**:\n   - Same number of rows as input\n   - Survey ResponseID matches cleaned survey exactly\n   - One-to-one mapping per respondent\n\n## Implementation Approach\n\n```python\ndf = pd.read_csv('survey_cleaned.csv')\n\n# 1. Separate ID from features\nresponse_id = df['Survey ResponseID']\ndemo_features = df.drop('Survey ResponseID', axis=1)\n\n# 2. Convert binary features\nbinary_cols = ['col1', 'col2']\nfor col in binary_cols:\n    demo_features[col] = demo_features[col].map({'Yes': 1, 'No': 0})\n\n# 3. Not general-purpose forecasting - focuses on anomaly detection and ranking\n    - Not real-time detection - works with historical data for post-hoc analysis\n    - Does not handle complex multivariate time series\n  requires:\n    - upper) / std\n- If below lower bound: raw_index = (actual - lower) / std\n- Scaled Index = 100 × tanh(raw_index) → bounded to [-100, 100]\n  - 1σ → ~76 points, 2σ → ~96 points, 3σ → ~99.5 points\n\n## Key Prophet Parameters\n\n- `min_training_days`: Minimum historical days required (e.g., 180) - filter out categories below threshold\n- `confidence_interval`: Width for anomaly detection (0.68 = ±1σ recommended)\n- `changepoint_prior_scale`: Trend flexibility (0.05 default, 0.1 for smaller datasets)\n- `seasonality_prior_scale`: Seasonality strength (10.0 default)\n\n## Required Inputs\n\n- `date_col`: Temporal dimension column name\n- `category_col`: Grouping dimension (e.g., product category)\n- `value_col`: Metric to analyze (e.g., price, revenue)\n- `cutoff_date`: Training/prediction split date (YYYY-MM-DD)\n- `prediction_end`: End of anomaly detection window\n- `agg_func`: Aggregation method ('sum', 'mean', 'count', or None if pre-aggregated)\n\n## Expected Output Structure\n\n- DataFrame sorted by Anomaly_Index descending\n- Columns: category name, Anomaly_Index, summary statistics\n- Index range: [-100, 100] with both positive (surge) and negative (slump) values\n- At least 20 categories to support top-10 surge + top-10 slump selection\n\n# Execution Notes\n\n1. **Read task requirements fully** - Causal analysis of trend anomalies with demographic features\n    - Identifying drivers of spending changes across treatment vs baseline periods\n    - DiD with both intensive margin (how much) and extensive margin (how many) estimation\n  scope_out:\n    - Simple descriptive statistics without causal inference\n    - Time-series forecasting without treatment/control comparison\n    - Single-period analysis without baseline-treatment structure\n  requires:\n    - Cleaned demographic data with numeric engineered features\n    - Transaction data aggregated to user-category-period level\n    - Clear baseline and treatment period definitions\n    - Anomaly detection results identifying target categories\n    - Python with pandas, statsmodels for DiD regression\n  preferred_tools:\n    - pandas for data manipulation and aggregation\n    - statsmodels OLS/WLS for DiD regression with p-values\n    - Standard statistical libraries (numpy, scipy)\n  risks:\n    - Confusing intensive (purchasers only) with extensive (all users) margins\n    - Including non-participants in intensive margin dilutes effects\n    - Not creating complete grid for extensive margin biases rates\n    - Using manual calculation loses p-value significance testing\n    - Wrong baseline period selection (e.g., 2019 instead of immediate pre-treatment)\n  examples:\n    - Analyzing March 2020 spending surge/slump with demographic drivers\n    - Comparing intensive (spending per buyer) vs extensive (adoption rate) effects\n    - Identifying top 3 features explaining behavioral changes\n\n# Guidance\n\n## DiD Methodology Core Concepts\n\n### Intensive Margin vs Extensive Margin\n\n**Intensive Margin** - How much behavior changed among participants:\n- **Question**: \"Did buyers spend MORE per purchase?\"\n- **Data**: Sparse - only users who made purchases (excludes non-buyers)\n- **Outcome**: Continuous (total spending per user-category-period)\n- **CRITICAL**: Do NOT include zeros for non-purchasers\n\n**Extensive Margin** - How many people changed behavior:\n- **Question**: \"Did MORE people start buying?\"\n- **Data**: Complete panel - ALL users × categories × periods\n- **Outcome**: Binary (0 = no purchase, 1 = made purchase)\n- **CRITICAL**: MUST include non-purchasers\n\n### Data Preparation\n\n**For Intensive Margin:**\n```python\n# Only aggregate actual transactions - do NOT create complete grid\nintensive_df = transactions.groupby(['user_id', 'category', 'period'])['spending'].sum().reset_index()\n# Result: Only rows where purchases occurred (sparse)\n```\n\n**For Extensive Margin:**\n```python\n# Create complete user × category × period grid\ncomplete_grid = pd.DataFrame(\n    list(product(all_users, target_categories, ['baseline', 'treatment'])),\n    columns=['user_id', 'category', 'period']\n)\n\n# Mark who purchased\npurchases_lookup = transactions[['user_id', 'category', 'period']].drop_duplicates()\npurchases_lookup['has_purchase'] = 1\n\nextensive_df = complete_grid.merge(purchases_lookup, how='left')\nextensive_df['has_purchase'] = extensive_df['has_purchase'].fillna(0).astype(int)\n```\n\n### Regression Methods\n\nDiD regression: `Y = α + βX + γPost + δ(X × Post) + ε`\n- Interaction coefficient δ is the DiD estimate for feature X\n- Use statsmodels OLS/WLS to get p-values\n\n**Multivariate Heterogeneous DiD**: All features simultaneously, controls for confounding (use when n_observations ≥ n_features × 10)\n\n**Univariate DiD**: Separate regression per feature, more robust with small samples\n\n## Output Requirements\n\nFor each category (top 10 surge, top 10 slump):\n- **Intensive margin**: Top 3 features sorted by DiD estimate strength\n  - Surge: descending (largest positive first)\n  - Slump: ascending (largest negative first)\n- **Extensive margin**: Top 3 features sorted by DiD estimate strength (same logic)\n- Each driver: feature name, did_estimate, p_value, method\n\n## Period Definition\n\n- **Baseline**: Immediate pre-treatment (e.g., Jan-Feb 2020) - NOT distant history\n- **Treatment**: Anomaly window (e.g., March 2020)\n\n# Execution Notes\n\n## Critical Distinctions\n\n1. **Intensive requires SPARSE data** (purchasers only) - including non-purchasers changes the question\n2. **Extensive requires COMPLETE panel** (all users) - missing non-purchasers biases rates\n3. **Filter to target categories BEFORE DiD** - only top-N anomalous categories\n4. **Use regression, not manual calculation** - to get p-values and significance\n\n## Common Mistakes\n\n- Creating complete grid for intensive → Wrong, dilutes effects\n- Only including purchasers for extensive → Wrong, can't measure adoption rates\n- Analyzing all categories instead of filtering → Wastes computation\n- Using 2019 as baseline instead of immediate pre-treatment → Weakens causal identification\n- Not sorting drivers by magnitude → Returns arbitrary features\n\n## Verification Steps\n\n1. Intensive has fewer rows than extensive (purchasers vs all users)\n2. Extensive has exactly: n_users × n_categories × n_periods rows\n3. No duplicate user-category-period in either dataset\n4. Period labels match exactly between datasets\n5. All features are numeric\n6. P-values in [0, 1] range\n\n# Derived Execution Layer\n\n## Key Requirements Before Execution\n\n- `survey_feature_engineered.csv` exists with numeric features\n- `user_category_period_aggregated_intensive.csv` exists (sparse, purchasers only)\n- `user_category_period_aggregated_extensive.csv` exists (complete panel)\n- `category_anomaly_index.csv` exists with anomaly scores\n- Period labels are \"baseline\" and \"treatment\"\n- No duplicate user-category-period combinations\n- Baseline defined as Jan-Feb 2020, treatment as March 2020\n\n## Expected Outputs After Completion\n\n- `causal_analysis_report.json` created\n- Valid JSON structure with metadata, surge_categories, slump_categories, summary\n- Exactly 10 surge categories (positive anomaly indices) and 10 slump categories (negative)\n- Each category has intensive_margin and extensive_margin arrays with 3 drivers\n- Drivers have: feature, did_estimate, p_value, method\n- Drivers sorted correctly (descending for surge, ascending for slump)\n- Baseline dates contain \"2020\" and \"01\"/\"02\", treatment contains \"2020\" and \"03\"\n\n## High-Impact Failure Modes to Avoid\n\n1. **Wrong baseline period**: Using 2019 or full 2020 instead of Jan-Feb 2020\n2. **Intensive contamination**: Including non-purchasers (row count equals extensive)\n3. **Incomplete extensive panel**: Missing non-purchaser combinations\n4. **Category filtering omission**: Running DiD on all categories instead of top 20\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}
